    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="b08104cb39584ea2991bdf504faf2e7d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b08104cb39584ea2991bdf504faf2e7d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '72b31a4c-498b-4414-999d-3f736602a8b9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1002/adfm.202314147&amp;type=tocOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3F"    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d147865899" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Exploiting the ability to directly deposit larger-sized, flexible, alloying-type sodium-ion battery electrodes without the need of additional inactive components and processing steps is a strategic way to achieve higher gravimetric capacities which will be suitable for commercial-level sodium-ion (SIB) pouch cell manufacturing. In this regard, a simple, template-free electroplating protocol is reported for designing phase-pure&nbsp;tin antimonide (TA)-based negatrode. An optimum control over the potentiostatic electroplating parameters allows the preferential formation of dendritic nanostructures over an electroplated foam-based current collector. The prepared negatrode and Mn-based Prussian blue analog positrode in full coin cell format deliver an average nominal working voltage of 3.0&nbsp;V with a discharge energy density of 342&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. In single-layer full pouch cell format, an 8.01&nbsp;mAh capacity (89.89&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 593.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) is achieved at an undeformed state and 97.12% capacity is retained at 180° bend condition. In multi-layer full pouch cell format, a 35.86&nbsp;mAh capacity (80.58&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 531.2&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) is achieved with 62.77% capacity retention after 200 cycles (0.125&nbsp;C). The results of this work showcase how a simple, template free, aqueous electrolyte based electroplating protocol can be developed for the direct fabrication of large-sized, flexible, alloying-type negatrodes for commercial SIB pouch cells.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314147-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the realm of alkali-ion rechargeable batteries, the time has come to consider replacing the current favorite, the Li-ion system. Sodium presents itself as an obvious substitute for lithium in rechargeable batteries, primarily due to their similar chemical properties. During the early days of lithium-ion battery (LIB) research, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) were also studied. However, such research studies were discontinued, primarily because of slower progress, and lower performance of SIBs compared to LIBs at the time. Nevertheless, as the global economy continues to expand at an unprecedented rate, so does the demand for cost-effective solutions. In the present day, renewed interest in sodium-ion batteries is primarily motivated by abundant availability of sodium and the hope of producing batteries that are not only cheaper than LIBs but also less susceptible to resource-related concerns.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0001_R_d147865891e363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A recent trend of transforming commonly-used technologies into the sustainable domain is also fulfilled as SIBs offer a much lower environmental impact than LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0002_R_d147865891e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The added advantages of SIBs include, but not limited to, the use of Al (aluminum) instead of Cu (copper) as the current collector, storage, and transportation at 0% SOC (state of charge), higher power density, and fast charging capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0003_R_d147865891e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thanks to efforts of Natron Energy and ABB Power Conversion, a Prussian blue analog (PBA) based SIB module comprising of 4.6 Ah sodium-ion cells has appeared in the commercial market boasting high-rate capability and cyclability without thermal runaway issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0004_R_d147865891e379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Power Sources">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Now, it is essential that any proposed solution aligns with the motivation of ‘cost-cutting technology development’ along with the actual scope for commercial scalability. Numerous research papers on SIBs are being published on a daily basis, with each one consistently surpassing previous ones in performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0005_R_d147865891e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the transfer of these innovations into practical application is rather rare primarily due to unscalable fabrication techniques/tools implemented in these studies. On one hand, synthesizing active materials involves intricate processing steps and energy-intensive protocols. On the other hand, the fabricated electrodes themselves contain substantial amounts of “dead weight”, which refers to additional components that do not contribute to the capacity of the SIB. All of these issues become significantly amplified when attempting to transfer the proposed innovation to an industrially relevant level (for instance, comparing performance between coin cell and pouch cell).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0006_R_d147865891e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the aforementioned arguments, we decided to focus on developing a negative electrode, referred to as a “negatrode”, for SIBs that could genuinely hold real-world applicability.</p>
         
         <p>The current favorite among the list of negatrode candidates for SIBs (i.e., hard carbon), possess a critical flaw. This flaw is characterized by a low sodiation potential (close to Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) along with a relatively subpar cycling performance, which can potentially result in the formation of sodium metal plating and dendritic growth, thereby posing a safety hazard.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0007_R_d147865891e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In response, alloying-type materials such as tin (Sn, 847&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and antimony (Sb, 660&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) have drawn significant interest due to their substantial theoretical gravimetric/volumetric capacities with a conducive working potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0008_R_d147865891e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The combination of these materials into a tin antimonide intermetallic alloy (SnSb: TA) can further enhance the performance, as these alloys exhibit relatively smaller volume expansions during the sodiation process, leading to extended cycle life at higher rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0009_R_d147865891e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A Appl. Surf. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SnSb has a high theoretical capacity for sodium-ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0010_R_d147865891e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Res.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There has been significant research on SnSb in recent years, aiming to address challenges and enhance its performance. Researchers and industries are actively exploring ways to scale up the production of SnSb-based materials for use in sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0008_R_d147865891e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The commercialization potential is supported by efforts to optimize synthesis methods, improve electrode design, and enhance the overall performance of SnSb-based electrodes. This ongoing research contributes to the feasibility of commercializing sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <p>One can prepare intermetallic SnSb alloy using chemical reduction, hydrothermal/solvothermal treatment, and traditional solid-state methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0011_R_d147865891e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Solid State Ionics J. Power Sources J. Power Sources Ionics Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Alloys Compd.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, many of these techniques require high-temperature treatment, which can lead to improper phase formation due to variations in vapor pressure of alloying elements at an increased risk of oxidative contamination. Additionally, these methods often yield alloy nano-powders that necessitate additional processing, such as tape-casting, to prepare battery electrodes for electrochemical performance evaluation. On the other hand, the electroplating technique offers a distinct advantage by enabling direct deposition of metallic/alloy films onto a conducting substrate at room temperature. This eliminates the need for additional binders or post-processing of fabricated electrodes. Hence, thin, lightweight coatings can be directly fabricated, making electroplating suitable for applications where minimizing weight and thickness is critical, as is often the case with flexible electronics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0012_R_d147865891e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Electroplating allows for the deposition of a thin layer of metal onto the surface of a substrate, with scope for obtaining conformal coatings of intricate and complex shapes. Flexible electrodes often have intricate designs, and electroplating ensures that the coating adheres closely to the contours of the flexible substrate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0013_R_d147865891e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electroplating also allows for precise control over the thickness and composition of the plated layer, providing manufacturers with the flexibility to tailor products according to specific design requirements. This uniformity is crucial for flexible electrodes, as it ensures consistent performance and properties across the entire surface. Finally, electroplating processes can be easily adapted for mass production, making it suitable for industries that require large quantities of coated products. This scalability is a key factor in its commercialization potential. In a similar avenue, electrophoretic deposition also provides comparable advantages but is better suited for coating non-conducting electro-active materials derived from their stable colloidal solutions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0014_R_d147865891e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Lett. J. Alloys Compd.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Although several attempts have been made to electroplate pure-phase SnSb intermetallic without impurities, there has been no success. A significant breakthrough was reported by Prieto and co-workers, where they successfully electroplated pure phase SnSb using a deep eutectic solvent for SIB negatrode applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0015_R_d147865891e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lima and co-workers have also accidentally synthesized pure phase SnSb using an aqueous electrolyte while fabricating Sn<sub>x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub> films with varying composition for CO<sub>2</sub> reduction application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0016_R_d147865891e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ChemElectroChem.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in both cases, the morphology of deposits lacked geometric regularity. Finally, Li et&nbsp;al. were able to electroplate a SnSb intermetallic SIB negatrode in a pyramidal nanoarray format from an aqueous electrolyte via a strategic use of trisodium citrate and ethylenediamine hydrochloride as surface-directing agents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0017_R_d147865891e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, both SIB-based works were restricted to only coin cell level. Hence, in this work, we electroplated phase-pure intermetallic TA negatrode with dendritic morphology from an aqueous electroplating bath over a larger area suitable for pouch cells. To improve the cycling stability, we chose to electroplate the dendritic film over a 3D microporous Ni (nickel) foam which was also electroplated using a dynamic hydrogen bubble template (DBHT) method.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance was initially characterized in a coin cell format where a full cell configuration was made using a Mn-based Prussian blue analog (Mn-PBA) positrode. As a result, a discharge energy density of 342&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> was achieved at an average nominal working voltage of 3.0&nbsp;V. For proof-of-concept demonstration of the scale-up potential of the proposed strategy, single-layer pouch cell (SLPC) and multi-layer pouch cell (MLPC) (4.5&nbsp;cm&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;cm) in the same full cell configuration were fabricated and tested. The SLPC delivered a discharge capacity of 8.01&nbsp;mAh which translated into an aerial capacity of 593.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. When deformed, the SLPC delivered discharge capacities of 7.94, 7.84, and 7.78&nbsp;mAh at 45°, 90°, and 180° bend conditions, respectively, showcasing its excellent flexibility properties. Upon long-term cycling at 0.125&nbsp;C rate, the SLPC retained a discharge capacity of 5.81&nbsp;mAh after 200 cycles. The MLPC also delivered a discharge capacity of 35.86&nbsp;mAh (531.2 µAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) in the initial cycle and retained 22.51&nbsp;mAh after 200 cycles at 0.125&nbsp;C rate. These results showcase how a simple electroplating protocol can be used to directly prepare large-sized alloying-type negatrodes for SIBs and facilitate its practical application in pouch cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314147-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>As described in the experimental section, dendritic TA films were electroplated onto Ni foam and film-based current collectors to yield binder-free electrodes (denoted as TA@Foam and TA@Film, respectively) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1a</a>). According to the electrochemical series, reduction of Sn<sup>2+</sup> and Sb<sup>3+</sup> occured at potentials close to SHE (Sn<sup>2+</sup>/Sn at ‒0.13&nbsp;V and SbO<sup>+</sup>/Sb at +0.212&nbsp;V vs SHE, respectively) where sign of both reduction potentials were opposite to each other (in acidic pH).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0018_R_d147865891e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, achieving a co-deposition of Sn and Sb on the same electrode was thermodynamically infavorable. To circumvent this issue, a suitable complexing agent could be used to shift the reduction potential to a more pliable value.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0019_R_d147865891e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Chem. Educ.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this aspect, using citric acid as a complexing agent should impart a significant cathodic shift of the reduction potential to a more negative value with respect to a platinum counter. A similar effect of citrate ions has also been reported by Chraibi et&nbsp;al. who have attempted to electroplate CuInSe<sub>2</sub> ternary alloy thin films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0020_R_d147865891e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Phys. Status Solidi A">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1b</a> presents the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve (two-electrode CV analysis) which suggests that the reduction of both Sn<sup>2+</sup> and Sb<sup>3+</sup> occurs between ‒1.5 to ‒2.0&nbsp;V. As expected, the presence of citric acid molecules in the electroplating bath imparted two distinct phenomena: 1) Significant cathodic shifts in reduction potentials of the two metal ions (‒1.82&nbsp;V for Sn<sup>2+</sup>/Sn and ‒1.69&nbsp;V for SbO<sup>+</sup>/Sb); and 2) a narrow deposition potential gap (‒0.13&nbsp;V). Hence, the chance for the Sn-Sb co-deposition increased drastically, which in turn should promote the formation of the desired TA phase. For reference, we also carried out CV analyses for only-Sn and only-Sb containing electrolytes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>, Supporting Information). It was observed that individual reduction potentials of Sn<sup>2+</sup> and SbO<sup>+</sup> had a similar type of cathodic shift, although their values were different from that of the combined electrolyte (‒1.43&nbsp;V for Sn<sup>2+</sup>/Sn and ‒1.19&nbsp;V for SbO<sup>+</sup>/Sb). This proves that, in conjunction to the effect of citric acid complexation, both Sn and Sb complexes can also interact when they are present together in the same system leading to a much higher cathodic shift. Such an interaction can also prove beneficial in terms of achieving a specific deposit morphology. This will be discussed later in the following paragraphs. Pure-phase TA possesses a rhombohedral R‒3m crystal structure (ICDD 00-033-0118) and characteristics of an intermetallic compound (as predicted by the thermodynamic equilibrium binary phase diagram of Sn and Sb).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0021_R_d147865891e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a tendency lies in the TA phase that might allow the formation of dendrites. Dendrites are a type of superlattice structures that normally form during non-equilibrium undercooling of metallic melts. Intermetallic melts also show a strong tendency for dendrite formation via disordered crystal growth near the fast-moving solidification front during rapid undercooling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0022_R_d147865891e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Crystals">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Dendrites are commonly observed to have a structure similar to a compound leaf (primary stem with secondary branches). The thermodynamics behind the formation has been well explored and documented in scientific literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0022_R_d147865891e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Crystals">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0023_R_d147865891e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Alloys Compd. Mater. Today: Proc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Popov et&nbsp;al. have revealed that dendritic-type deposits can also be achieved via electrodeposition where one needs to apply a certain overpotential (beyond the reduction potential of the participating ions) for achieving the feat.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0024_R_d147865891e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By increasing the overpotential beyond a certain onset value for a specific time period, the possibility of obtaining a dendritic-type deposit increases manyfold as similar conditions tend to appear at the electrode-electrolyte interface mimicking those observed during metallic melt undercooling. We also employed a similar strategy by applying a significant overpotential (η) of ‒1.2&nbsp;V (deposition carried out at ‒3.0&nbsp;V vs CE) to obtain dendritic TA deposits. The current-time transient curve measured during potentiostatic electroplating of the TA dendritic films (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1c</a>) shows that a linear increase in the current occurs until 1000&nbsp;s of electroplating where Sn and Sb clusters nucleate on the surface of the substrate, which is confirmed by time-dependent scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1e,f</a>). This segment can be termed as “induction time of dendritic growth”, which is the time required to achieve the “critical overpotential” for obtaining dendritic deposits.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0025_R_d147865891e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This induction time is the minimum time necessary for the onsent of dendritic growth (i.e., the time in which the current density on the tip of the dendrite becomes equal to the limiting current density on the tip of a non-dendritic potrusion of the same height in the Helmholtz double layer present on the electrode surface). Beyond this induction time, the transient curve becomes asymptotic to the time axis as current values become more-or-less stable. This marks the point where the rate of growth of dendrites starts to supercede lateral-directed film growth and the electroplated morphology changes from a spherical-type into a dendritic-type. In our case, time-dependent SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1f,g</a>) taken before and after the transition zone (change in slope of the current vs time transient curve) confirms this concept. The morphology of the deposits obtained at 900&nbsp;s (before transition) and at 1200&nbsp;s (after transition) shows that the growth mechanism transforms from a spherical-type morphology into a dendritic-type while crossing the 1000&nbsp;s mark. Subsequently, at 1500&nbsp;s, proper dendrites appear throughout the substrate surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1h</a>). These optimization experiments described above were conducted directly on Cu (copper) foil to ensure that corresponding SEM images possess adequate clarity for observing the dentrite formation process. However, upon repeating the same over nickel foam and film, we did not observe any significant change in the electrochemical behavior of the dendrite deposition process. Hence, it can be safely asummed that the surface chemistry of the current collectors have minimal-to-no effect of the electroplating mechanism of dendritic films. Furthermore, the obtained dendritic deposits showed only the presence of pure-phase TA throughout the substrate, as confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1d</a>). No impurity peaks of Sn or Sb phases can be detected. On the contrary, control sample deposits obtained from only-Sn and only-Sb electrolytes show the presence of only pure-phase Sn and pure-phase Sb, respectively. A stark difference is also visible in the deposit morphology. Under the given electrolyte composition, the Sn and Sb containing electrolyte delivers dendritic deposits (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). However, in the only-Sn electrolyte deposit, only spherulite-type deposits are sporadically present throughout the surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information). Similarly, in the only-Sb electrolyte deposit, planar deposits can be seen with numerous surface cracks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information). In either case, there is no evidence of dendrite formation, though dendrite formation is reported to occur during the electroplating of dendritic Sn and Sb films in earlier reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0026_R_d147865891e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Solid State Electrochem. Electrochim. Acta">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The plausible reason behind this is that the electrolyte composition used here (choice of complexing agent, pH of electrolyte, etc.) only favours the deposition of TA dendrites without allowing the deposition of pure tin/pure antimony nanostructures. This also indirectly ratifies the above discussed concept of how choosing a proper complexing agent can induce shifts in the reduction potentials of any metallic species when needed. It also sheds light on how two-or-more complexed metallic ions can interact with each other for achieving a specific deposit composition/morphology with proper selectivity. Incorporating additional porosity into bulk topography of dendritic TA films is expected to further improve electrolyte permeability of the electrode while simultaneously reducing the mean-free-path of electron and ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0027_R_d147865891e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we chose to electroplate dendritic TA films onto Ni foam as well as Ni film based current collectors for comparison. Fabrication of copper-based nanostructured foams have been reported previously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0028_R_d147865891e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although they are an obvious choice for our target application (copper current collectors used for anodes in Li/Na-ion batteries), we still decided to proceed with a nickel-based foam that can be electroplated on the copper foil. The reason behind this is that, based on literature survey, nickel fares better in accommodating the volumetric fluctuation stress occuring in alloying-type anodes during cycling, thus preventing delamination and improving columbic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0029_R_d147865891e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the costlier nature of Ni compared to Cu, utilizing a thin layer of Ni (over the current collector) is sufficient to achieve the desired effect without any significant increase in the fabrication cost. Cu foam is highly susceptible to oxidation when exposed to the ambient atmosphere due to its increased surface area. This susceptibility would elevate the interfacial resistance between the Cu foam and the TA film, necessitating the need for an inert atmosphere during handling, thereby reducing cost-effectiveness. Additionally, the mechanical strength of Cu foam is poorer than that of Ni foam, which might not be sufficient to tolerate the volumetric fluctuation stresses generated during the (de)sodiation of TA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0028_R_d147865891e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a>, <a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0030_R_d147865891e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Mater. Sci. Technol.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> One can advocate for Al (aluminium) foam as another alternative to Ni due to its ultra-low cost and light-weight. However, electroplating Al foam from aqueous electrolytes is not possible.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0006_R_d147865891e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electroplating Al itself is rather difficult and requires the use of non-aqueous organic or molten salt electrolytes. Since our target is to provide a technical solution that harnesses electroplating in a cost-effective manner, electroplating Al foam defeats the purpose by increasing processing costs. Furthermore, the mechanical strength of Al foam is also less than that of Ni foam. Hence, these arguments collectively suggest that Ni foam is a wise choice. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a> (Supporting Information) compare the potential/current versus time transients measured during electroplating of the Ni foam and Ni film on copper substrate. During the potentiostatic deposition of Ni foam, the applied potential remains constant at ‒8.0&nbsp;V while the measured current increases as the deposition progresses. Whereas, during the pulse-galvanostatic electroplating of Ni film (pulsed current density of 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), the measured potential abruptly decreases from ‒0.76 to ‒0.68&nbsp;V within 60&nbsp;s and then becomes relatively stable for the next 240&nbsp;s. Since the total consumed charge values are almost identical for both the electroplating processes, the difference in the morphology of the coatings obtained is attributed to the different underlying electroplating mechanisms. For the potentiostatic route, a high potential of ‒8.0&nbsp;V is maintained throughout the process and a foam type structure is formed rapidly. There is a rapid evolution of H<sub>2</sub> during the process which initiates a DBHT phenomenon that results in the formation of a foam type structure. On the other hand, during the pulse-galvanostatic route, the potential abruptly decreases from ‒0.76 to ‒0.68&nbsp;V within initial 60&nbsp;s and then becomes relatively stable for the next 240&nbsp;s. This suggests that a rapid formation of compact Ni nucleates on the working electrode occur during the initial 60&nbsp;s. After 60&nbsp;s of deposition, the rise in the potential suggests that lateral growth has started to dominate over vertical nucleation. This leads to the formation of a compact Ni film structure with a smooth surface finish. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a> (Supporting Information) compare the surface appearance of the Ni foam and Ni film substrates, respectively, at the same magnification. A comparison of surface topography at higher magnifications is also presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). A hemispherical pore-based foamy architecture is observed in the case of the Ni foam sample (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Whereas, a smooth, flat and continuous surface topography is observed in the case of the Ni film sample (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c,d</a>, Supporting Information). The Ni foam architecture comprises of micron-sized pores with nearly-circular geometry that are uniformly distributed on the surface. The pores appear to be interconnected from within the porous structure. Most of the pores have an average diameter of 20&nbsp;µm and they are interconnected by struts with thickness of ≈10&nbsp;µm. A high surface area is ensured by this architecture that will lead to improved electrode-electrolyte contact which, in turn, will result in faster electron and ion transfer. In contrast, the Ni film sample shows minimal surface porosity. The corresponding elemental mapping of the foam and film surface shows the presence of Ni (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information). The corresponding energy dispersive X-ray (EDS) spectrum does not show the presence of Cu suggesting that Cu atoms from the underlying copper substrate have not diffused into the top Ni layer during the electroplating step. Lateral SEM images (taken perpendicular to the sample edge) given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a> (Supporting Information) compare the layer thickness of the two samples. In the case of Ni foam, micron-sized, stacked cauliflower-type micropillars are observed with an average height of 10&nbsp;µm over the underlying copper substrate. A magnified top view of these hierarchical stacked-cauliflower architecture is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5d</a> (Supporting Information). In contrast, a smooth and continuous film with an average thickness of 3&nbsp;µm can be observed in the case of the Ni film. A magnified top view of the smooth continuous film is also presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6d</a> (Supporting Information). Both the foam and the film appear to be thoroughly anchored to the underlying copper substrate. Since, the foam and the film are going to serve as the electron-transfer medium between the electroactive TA dendrites and the copper substrate, their strong adhesion to the substrate is vital. The introduction of Ni foam will increase the dead weight of the anode. However, a trade-off is under consideration in this argument. To enhance the gravimetric capacity of the full cell, minimizing components such as binders and other non-active materials, often referred to as “dead weight,” is the preferred approach. Nevertheless, the incorporation of Ni foam is crucial as reports suggest that using a foamy current collector enhances the longevity of alloying-type negatrodes compared to a flat current collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0031_R_d147865891e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources Adv.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, using Ni foam, even at the cost of sacrificing some gravimetric capacity, is deemed acceptable as long as a balance between this trade-off and longevity can be achieved. We optimized the thickness of the Ni foam to keep it at a minimum, enough to contain the TA layer within its foamy architecture without significantly increasing the overall weight of the electrode (including Cu foil). In other words, the amount of Ni foam is kept the same as the amount of TA in terms of coating thickness. Electroplated coatings typically exhibit strong adhesion to the substrate. This is crucial for flexible electrodes, as they may undergo bending and stretching during use. A securely adhered coating ensures durability and reliability over the flexible substrate's lifespan. To verify adhesiveness of the Ni foam/film to the copper substrate, a cello-tape pull-out adhesion test is performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The absence of any coating material sticking on the cello tape after the pull-out test confirms the good adhesive property of the Ni foam/film layer with the underlying copper substrate. If the interfacial adhesion between the Ni layer (foam and film) and the copper substrate is not adequate, then delamination at the copper-nickel interface might occur during electrochemical cycling of the battery electrodes leading to capacity fade. Hence, a proper comparison between the foam and film in terms of stabilizing the electrochemical cycling of the dendritic TA films cannot be carried out. We studied the surface morphologies of electroplated dendritic TA films on Ni foam and Ni film based substrates. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2a</a> presents surface topography of the dendritic TA film electroplated over the Ni foam based current collector. The average pore diameter appears to have reduced due to the dendritic coating of the tin antimonde coating on the walls of the pores. Similarly, a homogeneous distribution of dendrites over the surface of the Ni film sample is also observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2d</a>). At higher magnification, we can observe that the dendrites are randomly oriented, although they are mostly perpendicular to the attached surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2b,e</a>). At the highest possible magnification (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2c,f</a>), SEM images show that the shape and size of the dendrites are not affected by the topography of the substrate (i.e., both present identical dendrite morphology). Upon comparing the SEM images taken at different magnifications, it can be concluded that the dendritic TA layer mimics the topography of the underlying Ni film/foam (when observed at a micrometer level) with an identical dendritic structure (when observed at a nanometer level). Primary backbones of dendrites are ≈800&nbsp;µm in length with secondary branches ranging between 50 to 200&nbsp;µm in length. Such a high aspect-ratio is expected to provide sufficient electro-active sites that will favour faster sodium-ion shuttling. High resolution tranmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images also confirm the structural aspects of TA dendrites. An interconnected geometric arrangement of dendrites can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2g</a>. A magnified view of a single dendrite shows the presence of two parallel backbones running along the central axis that jointly hold the secondary armlets in place, resembling an X'mas tree (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2h</a>). The lattice fringe analysis conducted at one of the secondary arms of a single dendrite shows the presence of (101) planes of the rhombohedral TA intermetallic phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2i</a>). A complete dendrite “megastructure” (composite leaf type) is observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2j</a> where EDS elemental mapping reveals a homogeneous distribution of Sn and Sb atoms. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3a,f</a> compare the surface-view and cross sectional view of the TA dendrite film electroplated over Ni foam along with corresponding EDS elemental maps. The presence of Sn, Sb and Ni can be clearly observed in electroplated dendritic samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3b–d</a>). Additionally, the cross sectional view also reveals the presence of the underlying Cu current collector which also confirms that Cu atoms have not reached the TA layer by diffusing through the Ni layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3g–k</a>). Thus, Cu contamination in the TA layer is avoided. Instead, the Cu diffusion is limited to the Cu─Ni boundary. Such a diffusion process gives rise to the formation of a possible Cu─Ni binary alloy from strong anchorage of the Ni layer. Similarly, at the dendrite-foam interface, some diffusion of Ni protruding into the top SnSb dendrite layer can be observed. Hence, a possible ternary Sn─Sb─Ni alloy is expected to be present at the interface whose content is relatively small to be detected in the XRD scans. This ternary alloy can act as an “alloy glue” contributing additional adhesive strength for binding the dendrite layer to the Ni foam/film surface and improving electron transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0032_R_d147865891e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Commun.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> An overall homogeneous deposit can be seen during visual inspection of the prepared electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3e</a>). A similar homogenous deposit can also be seen in the case of the Ni film (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3l,m</a> show a magnified and highly magnified region of the bottom part of a TA dendrite megastructure, respectively. Unlike Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2i</a>, we observed the presence of two new phases near the bottom boundary region. The lattice fringe measurements from the two marked regions show the presence of (510) planes of orthorhombic NiSn (ICDD 00-026-1289) (Region 1) and (220) planes of monoclinic Ni<sub>5</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> (ICDD 00-032-0041) (Region 2), respectively, which confirm the presence of alloy glue. The EDS line scan conducted perpendicular to the cross section of the TA@Foam electrode also provides evidence of the atomic diffusion that occurs during the electroplating process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The diffusion of both Cu into the Ni foam layer and Ni into the SnSb dendrite layer can be clearly seen. Between the Ni foam – Cu foil boundary, an overlapping region of Ni and Cu is present which suggests that Cu atoms diffuse into the Ni foam during the first electroplating step. Similarly, between the SnSb dendrite – Ni foam boundary, an overlapping regoin of Ni and SnSb is also present which suggests that Ni atoms diffuse into the SnSb dendrites during the second electroplating step. These observations confirm why both the electroplated layers (SnSb and Ni foam) have a strong adhesion with the underlying Cu foil current collector. Further qualitative and quantitative analyses of the electroplated samples were performed using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a> (Supporting Information) presents XPS survey scan of electroplated denditric TA films on Ni foam which shows the presence of Sn, Sb, C, and O. Ni peak is not present in the survey scan as the electroplated SnSb intermetallic layer is sufficiently thick to prevent the photoelectron excitation of the underlying Ni layer. The presence of C arises from surface-adsorbed citric acid moieties that was used as complexing agent during the electroplating step.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0033_R_d147865891e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of O arises from the partial oxidation of SnSb dendrite surfaces owing to their inherent high exposed surface area.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0034_R_d147865891e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Deconvoluted XPS spectra of Sn3d and Sb3d regions are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b,c</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Peaks appearing at 486 and 494.6&nbsp;eV arise from 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> photoelectron emissions of metallic tin atoms (Sn(0)),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0035_R_d147865891e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and peaks appearing at 530 and 539&nbsp;eV arise from the 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> photoelectron emissions of metallic antimony atoms (Sb(0)),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0036_R_d147865891e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. Some additional photoelectron emission intensity at 530&nbsp;eV is also contributed by the O1s emission that lies in close proximity of the Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> emission.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0037_R_d147865891e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical performance of the sodium-ion half cells fabricated using electroplated samples as electrodes were studied. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information) compare the chronopotentimetric profiles of the initial two cycles at a constant specific current of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (0.125 C rate) of Ni foam and Ni film based dendritic TA films (denoted as TA@Foam and TA@Film, respectively). We also tested the pure-Sn (denoted as T@Film and T@Foam) and pure-Sb (A@Film and A@Foam) deposited on both Ni film and Ni foam. We can observe that the first discharge capacity of the TA@Foam sample is 902&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, whereas the TA@Film sample delivers 793&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the same specific current of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This discrepancy in the first discharge values arises from the amount of SEI formation, which, in turn, depends on the surface roughness of the electrodes. The higher surface roughness of the TA@Foam electrode results in a higher amount of SEI formation compared to the TA@Film electrode. Hence, upon comparing the second discharge capacities of the two electrodes, we can observe that the irreversible capacity loss in the case of TA@Foam is 279&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> compared to only 121&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the case of TA@Film. The capacity loss is higher for the TA@Foam electrode compared to TA@Film electrode. As a result, TA@Foam delivers 623&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> whereas TA@Film delivers 672&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the second discharge capacity. This observation is possibly due to the entrapment of a few TA dendrites (that are present on the topmost surface) inside the thicker SEI layer of the TA@Foam electrode. Evidence of this can be found in the XPS analysis of the SEI layer on the cycled electrode which is discussed later in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7</a>. These trapped dendrites then loose electrical contact with the rest of the electrode and thus become inactive. As a result, there is a small decrease in the active material content of the TA@Foam electrode leading to a slightly lower second discharge capacity. The chances of such an entrapment of the TA dendrites are relatively low in the case of TA@Film electrode, as the formed SEI layer is relatively thinner. Hence, the TA@Film is able to deliver a slightly elevated second discharge capacity compared to the TA@Foam electrode. Nevertheless, the obtained discharge values are comparable to other recently-reported values for pure SnSb based electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0015_R_d147865891e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Commun.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0038_R_d147865891e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Power Sources Energy Technol. J. Alloys Compd. Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J. RSC Adv.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, like other reported works, these experimental values remain comparably lower than the expected theoretical capacity of SnSb intermetallic (753.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0039_R_d147865891e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Energy">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This deviation in theoretical and actual discharge capacities possibly occurs due to certain thermodynamic and kinetic roadblocks that restrict complete formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> as end reaction products. Instead, complex sodiation mechanisms result in the formation of a number of unreported structures that have been studied in-depth elsewhere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0040_R_d147865891e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therein, researchers have meticulously studied the exact mechanism of sodium (de)alloying in the SnSb phase and have identified the exact sequence of sodiation and desodiation alloying events that occur when a SnSb phase reacts reversibly with sodium. Since this mechanism is well-established in scientific literature, we used the reported potential values for comparison and analysis. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0003">3f</a>, the thickness of the electroplated dendritic TA layer is ≈9&nbsp;µm. Therefore, based on the electrode mass loading of 1.1&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, the areal capacity of the TA@Foam electrode translates into 685.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and the corresponding volumetric capacity translates into 761.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>. CV and dQdV versus V curves of the TA@Foam sample, presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4b,c</a>, show comparable sodiation peak positions that can be ascribed to different alloying reactions of Sb and Sn with sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0040_R_d147865891e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Initially, sodium forms an alloy with antimony present in SnSb phase, producing Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. Meanwhile, small regions of pure Sn phase separate from SnSb grains. Subsequently, these Sn regions combine with more Na to generate NaSn. As the sodiation of Sn continues, NaSn absorbs even more Na and converts into Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. Finally, during final sodiation of Sn, formation of orthorhombic Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is seen. These sequence of sodiation reactions occur in reverse during the subsequent charging cycle. In this case, during the initial cathodic sweep, a small peak emerges ≈0.74&nbsp;V, and disappears in the subsequent anodic sweep. This peak reflects the formation of a solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) assisted by the presence of fluoroethylene carbonate additive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0041_R_d147865891e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In subsequent CV scans, two cathodic and anodic peaks are observed. The cathodic peak at 0.53&nbsp;V is likely linked to alloying of Na with Sb to create Na─Sb, while the one at 0.24&nbsp;V may be attributed to alloying of Na with Sn. Further alloying of Sn with Na results in the appearance of a cathodic peak at a lower potential, close to 0&nbsp;V. During the subsequent anodic sweep, peaks at 1.11, and 1.26&nbsp;V correspond to the dealloying processes of Na─Sn and Na─Sb, respectively. The dQdV versus V plots also show similar peaks to that of CV curves but with a shift toward lower values. Such a shift is expected due to the difference in the fundamental science behind the measurement techniques.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0042_R_d147865891e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Sci. Technol.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A similar pattern is also observed in the case of TA@Film sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b,c</a>, Supporting Information). During the initial cathodic sweep, the peak corresponding to the SEI formation can not be clearly distinguished which signifies that a lower amount of SEI formed compared to the TA@Foam electrode. Instead, two peaks centred at 0.52&nbsp;V and close to 0.01&nbsp;V appear due to the Na‒Sb and Na‒Sn alloying reactions, respectively. In the subsequent sweep, three cathodic peaks and four anodic peaks are observed. The cathodic peak centered at 0.59&nbsp;V arises from the Na‒Sb alloying and the 0.34&nbsp;V as well as 0.1&nbsp;V peaks arise from Na‒Sn alloying.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0043_R_d147865891e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Small Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, during the anodic sweep, peaks centered at 0.72, 0.91, 1.02&nbsp;V arise from the dealloying of Na‒Sn while the peak at 1.21&nbsp;V arises from the dealloying of Na‒Sb.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0044_R_d147865891e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the lower surface roughness, the TA@Film sample presents a CV curve where the individual peaks can be seen with additional clarity compared to the TA@Foam sample. Such a difference between CV curves appears due to the difference in the capacitive contributions. Foam-based electrodes offer a higher surface area and surface roughness that incorporate capacitive charge storage. As a consequence, the current response increases during CV which results in an increase of area under the curve as well as peak broadening. In the case of film-based electrodes, these capacitive contributions are less which leads to the appearance of sharp and prominent CV peaks. A similar observation is documented by Nurpeissova and co-workers in Ni<sub>3</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> intermetallic alloy anode deposited on Ni foam where the foam-based electrode gave broader peaks and higher area under the curve compared to the foil-based electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0045_R_d147865891e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater. Today Energy">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another major difference appears in the discharge-charge performance between the two variants manifests during long-term cycing stability tests. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4d</a>, the TA@Foam sample is able to retain 77.8% of its initial discharge capacity after 100 cycles whereas the TA@Film sample completely disintegrates at ≈50 cycles. Upon further continued long-term cycling, the TA@Foam sample is able to retain 389&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the end of 200 cycles. The pure-Sn (T@Film and T@Foam) and pure-Sb (A@Film and A@Foam) deposits also fare poorly compared to the TA@Foam sample. Regarding rate capability, the TA@Foam sample delivers an appreciable capacity retention of 304&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a high specific current of 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and is able to recover 86.5% of its initial discharge capacity after rate capability testing (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4e</a>). This enhanced rate performance can be attributed to prevention of particle agglomeration, efficient volumetric stress mitigation, and enhanced ionic and electronic conduction facilitated by Ni foam. Hence, we chose the TA@Foam sample for further studies. It is well documented that alloys, especially with suitable microstructures, offer improved resistance against the volumetric fluctuation stresses during repeated (de)sodiation compared to the individual metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0046_R_d147865891e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, alloy nanostructures offer shorter diffusion distance and, in some cases, a scope for the formation of metastable phases which work in-tandem in increasing the cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0047_R_d147865891e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, using a specific alloy configuration, one can achieve a higher gravimetric capacity spread over a range of alloying reactions which distributes the generated stresses over a range of working potential. This strategy, termed as active-inactive strategy, ensures that one segment of the alloy remains inert while the other undergoes (de)sodiation. During this, the inert component helps in enduring some of the generated stress via plastic deformation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0048_R_d147865891e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Alloys Compd.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In our case, since Sn and Sb react independently with sodium at different potentials, the inactive component buffers the stresses generated by the active component and vice versa depending on the potential. This makes it relatively easier for Ni foam to buffer the stress generated in the case of SnSb alloy (TA@Foam) compared to pure Sn (T@Foam) and pure Sb (A@Foam) electrodes. We have already demonstrated that only the TA@Foam electrode contains dendritic morphology whereas the T@Foam and A@Foam electrodes lack dendritic morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Hence, all the above arguments allow the TA@Foam electrode to deliver an overall better performance than T@Foam and A@Foam electrodes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4f</a> illustrates Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) Nyquist plots of the TA@Foam measured intermitently during cycling. Corresponding simulated values obtained through curve fitting of equivalent Randle's circuit are summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The Randle's circuit comprises of a resistance component, a complex impedance component and a constant phase element connected in series. The resistance component represents electrolyte resistance (R<sub>elec</sub>) demarking the starting point of the initial semi-circle observed in the Nyquist plot. The complex impedance component comprises of a constant phase element (denoted as Q<sub>1</sub>), connected in parallel with a series combination of a resistance (denoted as R<sub>CT1</sub>), and a capacitor component (denoted as C). The other constant phase element is denoted as Q<sub>2</sub>. R<sub>CT1</sub> represents contact resistance between TA dendrites and Ni foam, while Q<sub>1</sub> and Q<sub>2</sub> represent the charge transfer reactions taking place at the TA dendrites and the dendrite-foam interface, respectively. R<sub>elec</sub> values suggest that the electrolyte resistance minimally increases as a result of cycling. Notably, the capacitance value experiences a ten-fold increase, suggesting that the active material undergoes amorphization during cycling. Previous studies have reported that repetitive (de)sodiation can generate immense volumetric fluctuations and stresses (expansion of 424% for Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and 390% for Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, respectively) due to the larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> ions compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0049_R_d147865891e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a consequence, Sb and Sn phases undergo lattice fragmentation during (de)sodiation steps leading to formation of amorphous intermediate phases with short-range ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0040_R_d147865891e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation contradicts the general trend where the thermodynamically stable phases (as outlined in the Na‒Sb and Na‒Sn phase diagrams) are expected to form.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0021_R_d147865891e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The preferential formation of these amorphous phases occurs due to the sluggish mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in short-range ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0050_R_d147865891e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these amorphous phases exhibit an atomic arrangement similar to the empirical formula of the phases predicted in the phase diagrams. Evidence of such an amorphization event occuring in this case can be seen by observing changes in exponent values of Q<sub>1</sub> (associated with Na alloying of SnSb). The exponent continuously increases toward 1.0, indicating a capacitive transition of the system. Such a transition can only occur when amorphization-induced disintegration of the dendrites occurs into smaller particulates (grains). A similar change can also be observed in the case of Q<sub>2</sub> where, due to amorphization, breakdown of the dendrite architecture leads to disintegration of the dendrite-foam. Confirmatory evidence of the above can also be deduced from post-cycling microscopic analysis which will be discussed later. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5a</a> presents the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of individual half-cells (mass-balanced) and full coin-cell (CC), recorded at a 0.125 C rate (0.015&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The charge capacity of Mn-PBA positrode is 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while the corresponding discharge capacity of TA@Foam negatrode is 623&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Thus, the anticipated theoretical capacity of full CC is estimated to be 121&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in this case considering active weights of both the electrodes. The prepared full CC demonstrates a charge capacity of 117&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, (equivalent to 96.6% of the predicted capacity) and a discharge capacity of 114&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in an coulombic efficiency of 97.4%. During the charge–discharge cycling, the full CC presents a 3.0&nbsp;V average nominal working voltage plateau indicating a discharge energy density of 342&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.125 C rate (0.015&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, the full CC exhibits a discharge capacity retention of 65.7% after undergoing 200 charge–discharge cycles at a rate of 0.125&nbsp;C (0.015&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5b</a>) where a coulombic efficiency of &gt;97% is maintained throughout the experiment. Under high rates, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5c</a>, the full CC delivers a specific capacity of 50&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (8 C rate, 0.96&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), indicating a high-rate energy density of 150&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Following high-rate cycling tests, the full CC recovers 88% of its initial capacity when the rate is lowered back to 0.125 C (0.015&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Overlapping CV curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information) of the full CC also indicate that Na<sup>+</sup> shuttling reactions within the full CC exhibit excellent reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0051_R_d147865891e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Eng. J.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> EIS Nyquist plots for the full CC are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a> (Supporting Information). The measurements were carried out in the discharged state of the full CC after 5 and 200 charge–discharge cycles. Equivalent Randle's circuit fitting parameters are summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The equivalent circuit appears similar to that of the TA@Foam half cell with a slight modification. In the second constant phase element, another charge transfer resistance component R<sub>CT2</sub> is added in parallel configuration which possibly describes the electro-kinetics of the positrode. The arguments made in the case of half cell hold true in the case of full cell as well. The electrolyte resistance minimally increases as a result of cycling. The C<sub>1</sub> capacitance value experiences a tenfold increase due to amorphization of the negatrode. Both the Q<sub>1</sub> and Q<sub>2</sub> constant phase elements exponents approach the value of 1.0, suggesting a capacitive transition. After achieving a successful test of the full cell configuration in a coin cell format, we went forward in upscaling the same in a pouch cell (PC) level. Over the past decade, significant research efforts have been dedicated to comprehensive examination and documentation of all major components such as active materials, binding agents, conductive additives, electrolytes, and separators. However, due to resource and cost constraints, many research laboratories continue to employ small-format cells, such as coin cells, for their evaluations. Thus, it is imperative to prepare and test electrochemical cells ideally using the same form-factor as commercially produced cells from production lines, i.e., in pouch, cylindrical, and prismatic formats, to get more reproducible results. We strongly believe in the need for proper evaluation of active materials of a new battery technology atleast at a pouch cell level to showcase its actual practicability which mostly stays hidden during simple coin cell level testing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0052_R_d147865891e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Commun. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The inset photograph of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5a</a> presents the positrode and the negatrode used for assembling the pouch cell (4.5&nbsp;cm&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;cm, 13.5&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>). Two different PC configurations were prepared viz., a single layer pouch cell (denoted as SLPC) comprising of a single pair of positrode and negatrode and a multi layer pouch cell (denoted as MLPC) comprising of five pairs of positrodes and negatrodes. The SLPC, owing to its thin form-factor, can be easily subjected to bending tests.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0053_R_d147865891e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem. Eng. J.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, we carried out electrochemical tests of the SLPC during mechanical deformation and the corresponding charge–discharge curves are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5d</a>. Under a pristine condition (no deformation), the SLPC delivers a discharge capacity of 8.01&nbsp;mAh which translates into a 593.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> areal capacity. This capacity is roughly 74.28% of the anticipated theoretical capacity (10.78&nbsp;mAh). This reduced practical capacity is because of the high mass loading of positrode and negatrode, which leads to reduced conductivity and a longer diffusion pathway for Na-ions. When deformed, the SLPC delivers a discharge capacity of 7.94, 7.84, and 7.78&nbsp;mAh at 45°, 90°, and 180° bend conditions, respectively. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5d</a>, the capacity of the SLPC reduces from 8.01 to 7.78&nbsp;mAh at 180° bent condition compared to its pristine state. This accounts for a 2.9% capacity decay which falls within tolerable limits. Such a decay can possibly be due to an increase in the internal resistance of the cell, especially around the bend region (middle part of the electrode). At the site of the bend, localized mechanical strain gets generated that hampers the electronic and ionic mobility in the area leading to minor capacity decay. However, the capacity is regained back when the deformation is removed. We carried out structural examination of a TA@Foam negatrode via visual inspection, optical microscopy and scanning electron microscopy to check the electrode integrity after bending (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). During visual inspection, we did not observe any major structural damage to the electrode after bending. However, small perturbations and surface cracks can be seen at the bend region when observed under an optical microscope. Under SEM, these surface cracks become more prominent, but the Ni foam architecture and the TA dendrites appear to be intact. Hence, we can conclude that bending the SLPC imparts a small amount of damage to the negatrode macrostructure, especially at the site of bending. However, the foam and the dendritic microstructure remain unaffected.
