The inset image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5d</a> and Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) show that the SLPC can continuosly light a blue LED while being deformed. Upon long-term cycling at 0.125 C rate, the SLPC is able to deliver 5.81&nbsp;mAh discharge capacity retention after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5e</a>). This accounts for a capacity retention of 72.53% in the case of the SLPC. The inset image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5e</a> also shows intact nature of the SLPC after consecutive mechanical deformation tests and long-term cycling tests. In the same avenue, MLPC also delivers a discharge capacity of 35.86 mAh (531.2 µAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) in the initial cycle and retains 22.51&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;after 200 cycles at 0.125 C rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5f</a>). This accounts for a capacity retention of 62.77% in the case of the MLPC. Due to thicker form factor of the MLPC (inset Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0005">5f</a>), conducting mechanical bending tests is not possible. Hence, we did not proceed in conducting electrochemical tests of the MLPC during mechanical deformation. The SLPC has a total active mass loading of 89&nbsp;mg whereas the MLPC has a total active mass loading of 445&nbsp;mg. Therefore, the SLPC's specific discharge capacity becomes 89.89&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (based on total active mass loading of a single pair negatrode and positrode). Similarly, the MLPC's specific discharge capacity becomes 80.58&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (based on total active mass loading of five pairs of negatrodes and positrodes). Both SLPC and MLPC deliver an average nominal working voltage of 3.0&nbsp;V which is same as that of the CC. Hence, the working discharge energy density for SLPC and MLPC turn out to be 269.67 and 241.74&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Since both the PCs were cycled at a rate of 0.125 C, the corresponding discharge power densities turn out to be 2.15 and 1.93&nbsp;kW&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. From a commercial point-of-view, the inactive components must also be taken into account while reporting the electrochemical performance of a pouch cell. Since electroplating is implemented in fabricating the negatrodes, the weight of binder and conductive additive is completely negated. Thus, taking all the components (electrode materials, separator, electrolyte, tab and Al plastic casing) into account, the SLPC weighs 603.7&nbsp;mg whereas the MLPC weighs 2926.7&nbsp;mg. Therefore, the actual energy densities become 39.8 and 36.7&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, presenting a more practical scenario beyond Li-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0054_R_d147865891e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Energy Lett.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is commonly known that a thin electrode minimizes electrical pathways and promotes electrolyte infiltration, leading to minimal potential polarization delivering the best performance. As the areal loading rises, the electrode film thickens and the tendency to fracture and delamination from the current collector increases during long-term cycling. The majority of the published works willingly use low mass-loading to improve capacity retention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0055_R_d147865891e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nat. Commun.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon comparison with reported literature, the full cell capacity retention values appear low as we employ high active material loading (≈1.3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>) negatrodes. Combined with the mass-loading of the positrodes (4.1&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>), the overall mass-loading of the full cell reaches 5.4&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>, significantly higher than reported literature. We can potentially increase the capacity retention of the fabricated electrodes by using low mass-loading during performance evaluation. However, this will not provide an accurate representation as our primary goal is to develop an electrode that meets commercialization requirements at a pouch cell level. For post-mortem analysis of the TA@Foam electrode (used in PC), XRD, SEM, and Raman characterizations were conducted. Comparing XRD patterns of TA@Foam before and after PC cycling, one can deduce that the TA intermetallic (SnSb) phase disintegrates upon prolonged cycling and phase-separates into individual Sn and Sb phases (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0006">6a</a>). The corresponding SEM images also corroborate the same observation. At lower magnification, a thick SEI layer can be seen covering the entire surface of Ni foam with portions protruding into the individual pores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0006">6b,c</a>). Several surface micro-cracks and depression can also be seen in the SEI layer. Long thread like features that can be seen sticking to surface of the electrode arise from glass microfiber separator used in PC. At higher magnification, the loss of dendritic morphology and the presence of agglomerated nano-particulates clinging to edges of the foam topography can be seen. These nano-particulates comprise of pure Sn and Sb grains as deduced from XRD measurements. These observations coincide with earlier reports as well as the argument presented in terms of the EIS spectra analysis regarding amorphization. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0006">6d</a> presents a schematic representation of the role played by Ni foam in prolonging the cycling stability of dendritic TA film. Ni foam present in TA@Foam sample successfully buffers the volumetric fluctuation stresses that is generated during reversible (de)sodiation of SnSb phase preventing delamination and loss of electrical contact (upto 200 discharge-charge cycles). In contrast, the planarity of Ni film in TA@Film sample is solely responsible for delamination of electro-active layers leading to capacity fade (beyond 50 discharge-charge cycles). Preventing the amorphization-cum-disintegration of dendritic structure, however, is way beyond the scope of the Ni foam because of the inherent nature of the process (i.e., crystal structure reordering). Several published works are already present in scientific literature where researchers have shown that by using Ni foam based current collectors, the electrochemical performance of alloying-type anodes can be improved significantly compared to flat current collectors in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0009_R_d147865891e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A Appl. Surf. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0027_R_d147865891e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a>, <a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0056_R_d147865891e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Mater. Today Chem. Chem. Eng. J. Small">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Researchers have used a variety of techniques to elucidate the role played by Ni foam in performance enhancement. Volumetric fluctuation stresses arising from repeated expansion/contraction of the alloying process is the primary cause of capacity fade in alloying-type anodes. Due to efficient stress buffering properties, Ni foam does not allow delamination of the active layer and loss of electrical contact. The foam architecture also provides faster pathways for electron conduction and prevents particle size agglomeration leading to shorter Li/Na ion diffusion pathlengths. Upon comparing the published data with our results, we can observe that Ni foam introduces similar features in our TA@Foam negatrode as well. Upon comparing Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0002">2a</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0006">6b</a>, we can observe that our electrode layer has not delaminated from the current collector even after 200 cycles. During EIS analysis, we can observe that there is an absence of Warburg element in the equivalent Randle's circuit used for fitting (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0004">4f</a>). The absence of Warburg suggests that no semi-infinite diffusion occurs in the electrode during cycling which proves that particle agglomeration is prevented by the Ni foam. During repeated (de)sodiation, any increase in particle size will cause the top layers of the agglomerated alloy particles to get sodiated first, thereby creating a composition gradient. These already-sodiated top layers then hinder the diffusion of subsequent Na<sup>+</sup> ions, that try to reach the inner un-sodiated layers. Therefore, semi-infinite diffusion processes start to occur leading to the manifestation of Warburg element. However, such phenomenon does not occur in the TA@Foam negatrode. Additionally, the order of magnitude of the constant phase element values as well as charge transfer resistance values do not change during cycling. This proves that the short diffusion lengths of Na-ions are maintained during cycling (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This combination of properties provided by Ni foam allows for an enhanced cyclability and rate capability performance of the TA@Foam negatrode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7a</a> presents the Raman spectrum of TA@Foam negatrode before electrochemical cycling. Upon comparing it with the Raman spectra recorded from bare Ni film and Ni foam samples, five distinct Raman peaks can be seen that originate from the chemical bonds/molecular environment of the dendritic TA layer. These individual peaks can be assigned to specific Raman active modes that are expected to be present in the TA system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0057_R_d147865891e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Phys. D">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A sharp peak is observed at 150&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> which corresponds to the asymmetric tetrahedral vibrations of Sb atoms. Two minor intensity peaks appearing at 300 and 650&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> arise from the asymmetric stretching modes of Sb─Sn─Sb bonds and Sn─Sb─Sn bonds, respectively. A combination of peaks appearing between 700 and 800&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> arise from the asymmetric stretching of Sn-Ni and Sb-Ni bonds that form at the interface of dendritic layer and Ni layer. The presence of these bonds confirm the presence of an “alloy-glue” which is expected to provide additional anchorage to the dendritic layer, allowing for a more sturdy adhesion. Finally, a couple of peaks appearing between 400 and 500&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> arise from symmetric stretching of the SnSb intermetallic alloy bonds in the radial breathing mode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0058_R_d147865891e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Mater. Today: Proc.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, a Raman surface mapping of the SnSb intermetallic active modes is carried out in a ≈200&nbsp;µm<sup>2</sup> area of TA@Foam sample at low magnification (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7b</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7c</a> shows the enlarged version of the Raman surface map where the specific regions of maximum Raman emission can be visually differentiated. Sporadic distribution of the high-intense Raman signals from the SnSb intermetallic active modes indicates the presence of localized SnSb dendritic grains in and around the edges of the Ni foam. However, things appear different in TA@Foam negatrode after cycling. In the cycled sample, asymmetric Sb─Sn─Sb stretching peaks, Sn─Sb─Sn stretching peaks and the symmetric stretching SnSb alloy bonds dissappear while asymmetric Sb─Ni and Sn─Ni stretching peaks remain (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7d</a>). These observations further confirm the amorphization phenomenon that is occuring in the negatrode. The SnSb phase disintegration is followed by phase separation of individual Sn and Sb resulting in the loss of the Sn─Sb─Sn and Sb─Sn─Sb bond signals. However, the Sn─Ni and Sb─Ni signals get retained suggesting that the dendrite-foam interface (alloy-glue) remains intact. The phase disintegration and phase separation phenomena can be visually seen in the corresponding Raman surface maps (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7e,f</a>). The sporadic distribution of the SnSb intermetallic active modes, which were present in the pristine negatrode, vanish in the cycled negatrode. Instead, a unifrom distribution of the pure-Sn and pure-Sb active modes can be observed throughout the scan area. Hence, it can be visually confirmed that the Ni foam is not able to prevent the amorphization-cum-disintegration of dendritic structure. Rather, it maintains the film-foam interface by preventing aggressive delamination of the active layer and loss of electrical contact. In order to understand the role played by the SEI passivation layer in the electrochemical performance of the TA@Foam, we conducted XPS analysis of the cycled TA@Foam negatrode. Based on the penetration power and excitation region of XPS technique (5–10&nbsp;nm), we can assume that the obtained spectra encompasses the photoelectron emission from the top SEI layer only.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0059_R_d147865891e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Electrochim. Acta">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the deconvoluted C1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7g</a>), peaks present at 282.7&nbsp;eV represent sp3 C─C/C─H bonds. Similarly, peaks at 284.3, 288.7, and 285.5&nbsp;eV represent ─CO, ─CO<sub>2</sub>, and ─CO<sub>3</sub> moieties, respectively. This suggests that the SEI passivation layer primarily consists of carbonated species (alkylcarbonates and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>). Peaks centered at 198.0 and 208.1&nbsp;eV in the deconvoluted Cl2p spectrum arise from the electrolyte salt (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) and its degradation product (NaCl) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7h</a>). The presence of F1s and Na1s peaks centered at 683.8 and 1070.4&nbsp;eV, respectively, confirms the presence of fluorinated species arising from the degradation of the fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7i,j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0060_R_d147865891e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Sb3d and Sn3d XPS peaks (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0007">7k,l</a>) show no shift in their binding energy values as compared to those of the pristine electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b,c</a>, Supporting Information) which suggests that they still remain in their elemental state even after long-term cycling. However, additional peaks at 535.5 and 496.4&nbsp;eV appear in the deconvoluted spectra which arise from Auger electron emission of Na atoms. These observations suggest that the formed SEI passivation layer is structurally stable and efficient in improving the cycle life of the TA@Foam electrode by providing adequate ionic mobility.</p>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314147-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3ac2e7a1-4b69-4a65-992a-55afedac9a15/adfm202314147-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3ac2e7a1-4b69-4a65-992a-55afedac9a15/adfm202314147-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/47586ef7-c021-4e9a-ac0a-8b20749f2d25/adfm202314147-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3ac2e7a1-4b69-4a65-992a-55afedac9a15/adfm202314147-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314147-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314147" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Raman spectra comparison of a) pristine and b) cycled TA@Foam and TA@Film negatrodes (pouch cell). Raman attached optical microscope images of b) pristine and e) cycled TA@Foam negatrodes with marked areas used for Raman mapping. Raman area maps of the c) pristine and f) cycled TA@Foam negatrodes. High resolution XPS spectra of the g) C 1s, h) Cl 2p, i) F 1s, j) Na 1s, k) Sb 3d+O 1s, and l) Sn 3d binding energies in TA@Foam negatrode after long-term cycling test.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314147-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Summarizing results of this work, a protocol for electroplating of phase-pure TA (SnSb intermetallic) negatrode with dendritic morphology from an aqueous electroplating bath over a larger area is reported. Using a similar protocol, a 3D microporous Ni foam was electroplated over Cu foil using a DBHT method prior to electroplating of the dendritic film. In half coin cell condition, the TA@Foam negatrode delivered a reversible discharge capacity of 623&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and retained 389&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.125&nbsp;C rate. Based on the mass loading and thickness of TA@Foam negatrode, the areal capacity translated into 685.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and the corresponding volumetric capacity translated into 761.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>. When coupled with a Mn-PBA positrode, the full coin cell delivered a discharge energy density of 342&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at a 3.0&nbsp;V average nominal working voltage. The full coin cell also retained 65.7% of its initial capacity after 200 charge–discharge cycles (0.125 C rate) with a sustained coulombic efficiency of &gt;97%. Under high-rate cycling, the full cell delivered a specific capacity of 50&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (8 C rate). In a single-layer full pouch cell format (single pair of positrode and negatrode), a 8.01&nbsp;mAh capacity (593.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) was achieved at an undeformed state. At 180° bend condition, a 7.78&nbsp;mAh capacity (576.3&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) was achieved. The single-layer pouch cell was able to retain 72.53% of its original capacity after long-term cycling (200 cycles, 0.125&nbsp;C). In a multi-layer full pouch cell format (five pairs of positrode and negatrode), a high capacity of 35.86&nbsp;mAh (531.2&nbsp;µAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) was achieved and the pouch cell was able to retain 62.77% of its original capacity after long-term cycling (200 cycles, 0.125&nbsp;C). Results of this work showcase how a simple, template free, aqueous electrolyte based electroplating protocol can be developed for direct fabrication of large-sized, flexible, alloying-type negatrodes for SIBs. Such a protocol offers an immense scope for industrial assimilation due to its simplicity, cost-effectiveness, and minimal-to-no post fabrication processing.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314147-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314147-sec-0070-title"> Electroplating Process of Metal Foam/Film Current Collector</h4>
                  
                  <p>A Gamry Reference 3000 potentiostat-galvanostat was used to carry out all electroplating steps as described below. Electroplating procedures were carried out in a ambient atmosphere at an average room temperature of 20&nbsp;°C. A schematic representation of the fabrication process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314147-fig-0001">1a</a>. Specifically, nickel metal foam was electroplated from an aqueous electroplating bath (pH&nbsp;=&nbsp;5.0) containing 0.01&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> nickel sulphate hexahydrate (SRL, 99%) and 0.1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> ammonium sulphate (SRL, 99%) using a procedure described elsewhere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0061_R_d147865891e1459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Briefly, a two-electrode potentiostatic electroplating protocol was used to fabricate Ni metal foams taking advantage of the DBHT method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0062_R_d147865891e1465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Metall. Mater. Trans. A">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A battery-grade Cu foil with an exposed area of 6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> was used as a working electrode and a graphite sheet with an exposed area of 6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> was used as a counter-cum-reference electrode. A negative potential of 8.0&nbsp;V was applied for 300&nbsp;s to facilitate foam formation. For comparison, Ni metal film coated Cu foil was also fabricated using a two-electrode pulsed-galvanostatic electroplating protocol from a standard Watts bath.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0063_R_d147865891e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Mater. Sci.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Watts bath (pH&nbsp;=&nbsp;4.0) comprised of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> nickel sulphate hexahydrate (SRL, 99%), 0.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> nickel chloride hexahydrate (SRL, 99%), and 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> boric acid (Sigma-Aldrich, 99.5%). It was heated to 45&nbsp;°C prior to the electroplating step. Like the case of Ni metal foam electroplating, a battery-grade Cu foil with an exposed area of 6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> was used as a working electrode and a graphite sheet with an exposed area of 6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> was used as a counter-cum-reference electrode. A pulsed current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (T<sub>on</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1&nbsp;ms, T<sub>off</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;9&nbsp;ms) was applied for 3*10<sup>4</sup> cycles (300&nbsp;s total plating time) to electroplate the Ni metal film. Finally, both the foam and film deposits were washed repeatedly with warm (60&nbsp;°C) deionized water and dried in a vacuum oven at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314147-sec-0080-title"> Electroplating Process of TA Dendrite Film</h4>
                  
                  <p>An electroplating bath (pH&nbsp;=&nbsp;4.5) containing 0.1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium citrate dihydrate (99%, SRL), 0.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> citric acid monohydrate (99%, SRL), 10&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> stannous chloride dihydrate (99%, SRL), and 10&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> antimony trichloride (99%, SRL) was used for the electroplating process. A two-electrode potentiostatic electroplating protocol was used. The Ni metal foam/film prepared earlier (Section 2.1, 6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>) was used as a working electrode and a graphite sheet (6.25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>) was used as a counter-cum-reference electrode. A negative potential of 3.0&nbsp;V was applied for 1800&nbsp;s to fabricate the dendritic film (mass loading ≈1.1&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). After the electroplating process, electroplated electrodes were washed repeatedly with warm (60&nbsp;°C) deionized water and dried in a vacuum oven at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314147-sec-0090-title"> Structural Evaluation of Electroplated Films</h4>
                  
                  <p>Surface topographies of TA dendrites were evaluated with a Zeiss GeminiSEM 500 FESEM with an acceleration voltage of 20&nbsp;kV. Elemental compositions were estimated with an AztecLive Advanced Ultim Max 100 EDS detector having a SATW Light Element Window attachment. Transmission electron microscopy imaging was carried out with a JEOL JEM-ARM200F CS-TEM at an acceleration voltage of 200&nbsp;kV. Crystalline phase analysis was carried out using X-ray diffraction measurements via Rigaku D/Max 2500&nbsp;V/PC XRD with Cu Kα radiation (λ&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.15&nbsp;418&nbsp;nm) at 40&nbsp;kV – 40&nbsp;mA X-ray tube condition in coupled 2θ mode (Bragg-Brentano geometry). X-ray diffractograms were measured between 20–80° 2θ with a step size of 0.02° 2θ and a time-of-step of 0.2&nbsp;s. They were indexed using star quality ICCD standard diffraction patterns obtained from PDF4<sup>+</sup> database (SnSb 00-033-0118, Sn 00-004-0673, Sb 00-035-0732, Ni 00-004-0850, and Cu 00-004-0836). Raman surface maps were measured using a Nanophoton RAMAN Touch Raman Spectrometer with a thermoelectric cooled front illuminated 1024 256 CCD detector and a 532&nbsp;nm Argon-Krypton gas laser excitation source. X-ray photoelectron spectra of dendritic films were obtained using a Thermo Scientific Theta Probe X-ray Photoelectron spectroscope with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source (1486.6&nbsp;eV). The survey spectrum was calibrated with respect to the C1s peak fixed at 284.8&nbsp;eV. Peak deconvolution of the XPS spectra was conducted with Fityk software.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0064_R_d147865891e1534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Peak positions were indexed using standard photoelectron emission data present in the National Institute of Standards and Technology X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy Database (NIST Standard Reference Database 20, Version 4.1).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314147-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electroplated dendritic TA films and pure-tin/pure-antimony films (both on Ni foam and Ni film substrates) were punched into 16&nbsp;mm diameter discs using a precision disc cutter (MTI MSK-T-06). Electrodes were then assembled into CR2032 coin cells (MTI) inside an Ar-filled glove box (Kiyon) with both O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O levels below 0.5&nbsp;ppm. Sodium-ion half cells were assembled using those 16&nbsp;mm punched discs as working electrodes and mold-pressed sodium metal discs (Sigma-Aldrich, average diameter of 16&nbsp;mm) as counter-cum-reference electrodes. Similarly, sodium-ion full cells were assembled using a manganese hexacyanoferrate positrode whose synthesis and properties were discussed elsewhere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314147-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314147-bib-0065_R_d147865891e1552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The as-prepared manganese hexacyanoferrate powder were tape-cast onto battery-grade Al foil along with conductive carbon black and carboxymethyl cellulose/styrene butadiene rubber (CMC/SBR, 50-50) binder in the ratio 5:4:1 by weight. The mass-loading of the positrode was kept ≈4.1&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for achieving a mass-balanced condition by optimizing the tape-cast process. An electrolyte containing 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Alfa-Aesar) dissolved in propylene carbonate (Sigma-Aldrich) with 5&nbsp;wt.% 4-fluoro-1,3-dioxolan-2-one (fluoroethylene carbonate, TCI Chemicals) additive was used. Whatman glass microfiber membranes (Grade GF/C) cut into 19&nbsp;mm discs were used as separators. Prior to the full cell assembly, initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) loss of the negatrodes was addressed by contact pre-sodiation technique. Sodium metal discs of appropriate sizes were pressed onto the electroplated negatrodes while being dipped into the electrolyte for 30&nbsp;min. Then, the negatrodes were carefully separated by peeling off the sodium metal discs so that the formed SEI layer remained undisturbed. The assembled CR2032 half cells and full cells were electrochemically tested using a Wonik PNE battery analyzer (SC 05-0.1). The galvanostatic charge and discharge (GCD) studies of the half-cell testing was carried out between 2.0 and 0.01&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Whereas the full cell GCD testing was carried out between 3.6 and 1.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out using the same Gamry Reference 3000 potentiostat-galvanostat used for electroplating dendrite films. EIS spectra were measured between 100&nbsp;kHz to 0.1&nbsp;Hz with a potentiostatic signal amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV. They were then analyzed using ZSimpWin 3.21 software.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314147-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314147-sec-0110-title"> Pouch Cell Fabrication and Testing</h4>
                  
                  <p>The best performing electrode composition (dendritic TA film on Ni foam) was chosen as negatrode and was coupled with manganese hexacyanoferrate positrode for pouch cell fabrication. For preparing the Ni foam modified current collectors, Cu foils with an exposed area of 25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> were used as a working electrode and a graphite sheet with an exposed area of 25&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> was used as a counter electrode. The negatrode active material was then electroplated on the Ni foam based current collectors. The obtained dendritic film mass-loading was slightly higher than that of the smaller-sized samples (Section 2.2) used for structural characterization and half-cell testing (≈1.3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). In response, the positrodes were prepared via tape-cast route (as described in Section 2.4) and the mass-loading was kept ≈5.3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (in response to the increased mass-loading of the pouch-sized negatrodes). Pouch cell electrodes of 13.5&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> (4.5&nbsp;cm&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;cm) were punched using a manual die-based electrode puncher (HSHP-300, Wellcos Corp.). Four millimeters width Al tabs (EQ-PlIB-ATC4, MTI) were welded ultrasonically onto the backside of the punched electrodes. For fabricating a single-layered pouch cell (denoted as SLPC), a single pair of positrode and negatrode were used. Similarly, for fabricating a multi-layered pouch cell (denoted as MLPC), five pairs of positrodes and negatrodes were used. Contact pre-sodiation of the negatrodes was also conducted to minimize the ICE losses and to achieve a mass-balanced condition. However, unlike in the case of coin full cell assembly, the negatrodes were kept in contact for 45&nbsp;min with flattened sodium metal foils while being dipped in electrolyte. Then, the electrodes were carefully aligned and sandwiched with glass microfiber membrane separators and sealed in Al plastic casing (EQ-alf-100-210, MTI) using a vacuum sealer inside the glove box. The same sealer was then used to seal the pouch cells after the addition of 150&nbsp;µl of electrolyte. The fabricated pouch cells were initially activated by GCD cycling between 3.5 and 2.5&nbsp;V at a low specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (combined mass of both positrode and negatrode active material) for 2.0 cycles to allow the equilibration of the electrodes and stabilization of the solid-electrolyte interface (SEI)/cathode electrolyte interface (CEI). Then, the activated pouch cells were subjected to regular GCD tests between 3.6 and 1.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), like that of coin full cell tests.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
